Here's what the search revealed:\n\n## Overview\n\nFollowing several incidents last year where lawyers faced admonishment for improperly using generative AI in court filings, judges across the country have issued standing orders addressing AI.(no content)Since Judge Brantley Starr of the U.S. District Court for the Northern District of Texas issued the first standing order on the use of AI in preparing court filings in 2023, hundreds of state and federal judges have amended or issued standing orders to regulate this technology.\n\n## Key Developments\n\n**Scope and Variation**: According to a 2023 study, nearly 75% of active federal judges have one or more judicial standing orders, with most applying to civil cases. The orders vary significantly in their approach, with some orders apply to AI generally while others focus specifically on generative AI.\n\n**Types of Requirements**: The standing orders typically fall into several categories:\n\n1. **Disclosure Requirements**: Requiring parties to disclose if GAI was used in any way, including legal research, during the preparation of the filing\n\n2. **Certification Requirements**: Requiring a party that uses GAI to generate a portion of a filing to attach a separate document disclosing the use and certifying the accuracy of its content\n\n3. **Complete Prohibitions**: Judge Michael J. Newman of the Southern District of Ohio prohibits any AI usage across the board with an exception for \"information gathered from legal search engines.\"\n\n## Notable Examples\n\n**California**: In California, 10 federal judges and one state court judge require disclosure and/or certifications of accuracy if AI is used to prepare court documents.\n\n**Illinois State Courts**: In December 2024, the Illinois Supreme Court issued its \"Illinois Supreme Court Policy on Artificial Intelligence\" which became effective January 1, 2025. The Illinois Supreme Court encourages the responsible and supervised use of AI and recommends that Illinois state court judges not require lawyers to disclose the use of AI in drafting pleadings.\n\n**Fifth Circuit**: In November 2023, the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit proposed a rule amendment that would require attorneys to certify that no AI was used in drafting filings or, if AI was used, that all generated text have been reviewed for accuracy. As a result, in June 2024, the Fifth Circuit announced that they would not adopt the proposed rule.\n\n## Current Trends\n\nAI use in all aspects in the practice of law is becoming more common, and it is likely that the implementation of new AI disclosure requirements is the emerging trend among states in federal and state courts.(no content)Despite growing concern, there is not (yet) any single, district, or state-wide local rule on disclosing the use of AI in court filings."}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"603e942a-3f7a-413d-a581-46ad4cf81985","uuid":"76857542-a226-4233-af7b-de91cbfc0867"}

Law Offices of Snell & Wilmer\",\"url\":\"https://www.swlaw.com/publication/the-eastern-district-of-texas-announces-planned-amendment-to-ai-related-local-rule/\"},{\"title\":\"Everything You Need to Know About the Texas Business Court's New Local Rules | Munck Wilson Mandala\",\"url\":\"https://www.munckwilson.com/news-insights/texas-business-court-new-rules-jurisdiction-ai-discovery/\"},{\"title\":\"Rules & Orders | Northern District of Texas | United States District Court\",\"url\":\"https://www.txnd.uscourts.gov/rules-and-orders\"},{\"title\":\"LOCAL CRIMINAL RULES Effective September 3, 2024\",\"url\":\"https://www.txnd.uscourts.gov/sites/default/files/documents/CRIMRULES.pdf\"}]\n\nBased on the search results, I found information about the Northern District of Texas local rule regarding artificial intelligence disclosure. Here's the full text of the rule:\n\n## Northern District of Texas Local Civil Rule 7.2(f) - Disclosure of Use of Generative Artificial Intelligence\n\nThe rule states that: \"(1) A brief prepared using generative artificial intelligence must disclose this fact on the first page under the heading 'Use of Generative Artificial Intelligence.'\" If directed by the presiding judge, the party must disclose the specific parts prepared using generative AI. \"(2) 'Generative Artificial Intelligence' means a computer tool (whether referred to as 'Generative Artificial Intelligence' or by another name) that is capable of generating new content (such as images and text) in response to a submitted prompt (such as a query) by learning from a large reference database of examples.\" \"(3) A party who files a brief that does not contain the disclosure required by subsection (f)(1) of this rule certifies that no part of the brief was prepared using generative artificial intelligence.\"\n\n**Key Details:**\n- The rule is codified as Local Civil Rule 7.2(f)\n- It became effective September 2, 2025\n- A similar rule also exists for criminal cases at N.D. Tex. Loc. Crim. R. 47.2(e) (effective Sept. 3, 2024)(no content)An attorney has already been sanctioned for violating this rule, with Judge Ed Kinkeade finding that Javier Perez violated the Northern District of Texas local civil rule that mandates the disclosure of generative AI use, highlighting that the court is actively enforcing this requirement."}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"603e942a-3f7a-413d-a581-46ad4cf81985","uuid":"10a0ee57-7728-4c15-99c8-71cc93fb4076"}

Heppner\n\nIn February 2026, U.S. District Judge Jed Rakoff of the Southern District of New York ruled that written exchanges between a criminal defendant and Anthropic's Claude AI platform were not protected by attorney-client privilege or the work product doctrine. The judge determined that no attorney-client relationship exists \"or could exist, between an AI user and a platform such as Claude\".\n\n## Case Background\n\nThe case involved Bradley Heppner, the former chair of bankrupt financial services company GWG Holdings, who was charged with securities and wire fraud and pleaded not guilty. After receiving a grand jury subpoena, Heppner used Anthropic's Claude on his own to map out his defense—generating 31 documents the FBI later seized from his home.\n\n## Legal Reasoning\n\nJudge Rakoff concluded that the AI-generated documents failed all three elements of attorney-client privilege: Claude is \"plainly not an attorney,\" there was no expectation of privacy given Anthropic's privacy policy, and the communications were not for obtaining legal advice from a lawyer.\n\n## Industry Response\n\nMore than a dozen major U.S. law firms have issued client advisories warning that conversations with AI chatbots like Claude and ChatGPT carry no legal protection when they touch legal matters, with some firms embedding warnings directly into client contracts.\n\n## Contrasting Ruling\n\nInterestingly, on the same day as Rakoff's ruling, U.S. Magistrate Judge Anthony Patti in Michigan ruled that a self-represented woman did not have to hand over her ChatGPT chats, treating them as her own personal \"work-product\" rather than conversations with a person.\n\n## Practical Guidance\n\nLaw firm Debevoise & Plimpton advised that if a lawyer directs a client to use an AI tool, the client should note this in the chatbot prompt itself, writing \"I am doing this research at the direction of counsel for X litigation\"."}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"603e942a-3f7a-413d-a581-46ad4cf81985","uuid":"53099c7e-c4fd-4576-a0e0-e1076a985d70"}
